Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/medium/am2c17789_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c17789&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Heteroatom doping is an effective method to improve the electrochemical properties of hard carbon anodes for sodium-ion batteries. However, the different roles of surface and bulk heteroatoms in Na storage have not been explored much. Herein, N, P dual-doped carbon nanofibers (NP-CNFs) with high doping contents and low surface area are designed to clarify the above issue. It is confirmed that P plays a more crucial role in Na storage compared with N. In addition, surface and bulk P not only possess different configurations but also show distinct Na storage activity. There are only oxidized PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups on the surface, which are inactive for Na storage but promote the stability of electrochemistry interphase, while in the bulk phase, unoxidized P–C bonds also emerge except PO<i><sub>x</sub></i>, which shows preeminently reversible Na storage activity, and the PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups are activated simultaneously. Furthermore, P-doping changes the reactivity of N-configurations with Na both on the surface and in the bulk phase, exhibiting interesting synergism. As expected, the surface stability, bulk activity, and synergism enable NP-CNFs to achieve superior performance. It could deliver a prominent capacity of 105.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 3000 cycles in half cells and 164.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles in full cells.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D56836%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShengyong%2BGao%252C%2BEnhao%2BLiu%252C%2BNing%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D51%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c17789%26title%3DNa%2BStorage%2BActivity%2Bor%2BInertness%2Bof%2BP-Configurations%2Bin%2BN%252C%2BP%2BDual-Doped%2BCarbon%2BNanofibers%253A%2BBulk%2Bvs%2BSurface%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D56846%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290765&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Binding energy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Binding%20energy" class="article__tags__link">Binding energy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291493&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanomaterials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon%20nanomaterials" class="article__tags__link">Carbon nanomaterials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290714&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Doping" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Doping" class="article__tags__link">Doping</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291009&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanofibers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanofibers" class="article__tags__link">Nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291354&amp;ref=fulltext" title="X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_X-ray%20photoelectron%20spectroscopy" class="article__tags__link">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=N%2C+P+Dual-doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_N%2C%20P%20dual-doping">N, P dual-doping</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na%2B+Storage+Mechanism&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na%2B%20storage%20mechanism">Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Bulk+Doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_bulk%20doping">bulk doping</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Synergistic+Effects&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_synergistic%20effects">synergistic effects</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Due to the rich resources of sodium on earth, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) exhibit prospective application prospects for large-scale energy storage applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Hard carbons (HCs) are regarded as a promising candidate for anode materials for SIBs due to their advantages of reversible capacity, wide source, and flexible designability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 2 and 3">(2,3)</a> However, the energy density and power density of HCs are still difficult to meet the practical application in SIBs. Heteroatom doping has been considered as one of the most effective strategies to improve the storage capacity and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of HCs because it can enhance electron transfer, provide additional ion storage sites, and modulate carbon layer spacing. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> Compared with the doping of a single kind of heteroatom, dual-doping with different heteroatoms, especially N and P dual-doping, is attracting increasing interest due to the synergistic effects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 7 to 13">(7−13)</a> Generally, it is thought that N introduces the “hole” defects in the carbon layer, while P-doping causes the “protrusion” defects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> In addition, P has a larger atomic radius and high electron-donating ability compared with N. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Considering their differences, N and P heteroatoms are expected to synergistically contribute their distinct advantages to optimize the Na<sup>+</sup> storage, which has been theoretically investigated. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Nevertheless, the development of high-performance HC anodes with accurately designed N and P configurations still faces great challenges. On the one hand, there is a lack of basic experimental understanding of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of N and P configurations both on the surface and in the bulk phase of HCs. As reported, the heteroatomic configurations on the carbon surface are usually different from those in the bulk phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> It is known that surface and bulk doping could play different roles in electrochemical applications. For instance, in the field of electrocatalysis, bulk doping mainly modulates the band structure of carbon materials near the Fermi level, while surface doping modifies the surface properties of carbon, such as acidity/basicity, wettability, and coordination ability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> However, the different roles of surface and bulk doping in alkaline-ion storage have generally been ignored until now. On the other hand, it is hard to prepare HCs with high N and P contents, which makes it difficult to distinguish different N and P configurations as well as their variations during the Na<sup>+</sup> storage processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Additionally, the preparation of N, P dual-doped HCs is mainly based on activators such as H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, etc., which can produce high specific surface area while introducing heteroatoms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> Although the large specific surface area facilitates the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>, it is an interference when revealing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of heteroatoms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> Consequently, it is of great significance to design high-concentration N, P dual-doped carbon with restricted specific surface area to clarify the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior and the synergistic effects of different N and P configurations both on the surface and in the bulk phase.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this paper, we employ polyacrylonitrile (PAN) and ammonium polyphosphate (APP) as precursors to prepare the N, P dual-doped carbon nanofibers (NP-CNFs-<i>x</i>, <i>x</i> is the mass ratio of APP/PAN), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. With the introduction of APP, the surface area and pore volume of NP-CNFs-<i>x</i> unexpectedly decreases compared with that of PAN-derived carbon nanofibers (N-CNFs) without APP. This provides a suitable platform for excluding the interference of surface or pore Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, elucidating the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior on heteroatoms and the synergistic effects of N and P doping. The P atoms on the fiber surface exist in the form of oxidized PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups, while in the bulk phase, the unoxidized P–C could also be observed except PO<i><sub>x</sub></i>. Surface PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups are redox-inactive for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, but they play a vital role in the stability of electrochemistry interphase. In the bulk phase, all of the P-configurations show extremely high Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption activity and reversibility. In addition, P-doping changes the activity and reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> storage on N-configurations both on the surface and in the bulk phase, exhibiting interesting synergism. P-doping also enlarges the interlayer spacing of carbon and improves the diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. NP-CNFs-3.0 can deliver a high reversible capacity of 329.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a high rate performance of 152.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which are prominently higher than those of N-CNFs. This work will help to design heteroatom-doped carbon with accurate doping sites and excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/medium/am2c17789_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration for the preparation process of NP-CNFs-3.0. (b) Optical photographs of NP-CNFs-3.0 fiber membrane. (c) SEM, (d) TEM, and (e) HRTEM images of NP-CNFs-3.0. (f) STEM image of NP-CNFs-3.0 and corresponding elemental mappings.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c17789&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Preparation of N, P Dual-Doped Carbon Fibers</h3><div class="NLM_p last">N, P dual-doped carbon nanofibers were prepared by electrospinning and carbonization using polyacrylonitrile (PAN) and ammonium polyphosphate (APP) as the precursors. Typically, 1.05 g of PAN (<i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 150,000, Sigma-Aldrich) was dissolved in 10 mL of <i>N,N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF, Sigma-Aldrich), and then 0.53, 1.05, 2.1, and 3.15 g of APP were added to the mixture separately. The solution was stirred at 50 °C for 5 h. Next, it was transferred into a 5 mL syringe with a steel needle. During electrospinning, the needle was 15 cm away from the collector. High voltages of 15 kV and −4 kV were applied to the needle and the aluminum foil collector, respectively. The injection speed was 0.2 mm·min<sup>–1</sup>, and the rotation speed of the collector was 100 rpm. The obtained precursors were dried overnight in an oven at 80 °C and then stabilized in air at 280 °C for 2 h. Carbonization was carried out in an inert atmosphere at 800 °C for 2 h. The obtained N, P dual-doped carbon nanofibers were denoted as NP-CNFs-0.5, NP-CNFs-1.0, NP-CNFs-2.0, and NP-CNFs-3.0 according to the mass ratio of APP/PAN. The undoped carbon nanofibers, denoted as N-CNFs, were prepared by the same method.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Preparation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO4)<sub>3</sub> (NVP)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For full batteries, the cathode materials, NVP, were prepared by the sol–gel method. First, 0.1 M NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> was dissolved in 80 mL of deionized water, and then 0.15 M NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, 0.15 M NaOH, and 0.1 M citric acid were added in the solution, which was then evaporated in an oven overnight. The obtained mixture was heated in an inert atmosphere at 800 °C for 9 h to generate NVP.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The surface morphologies of the samples were recorded with a Zeiss SUPRA 55 field emission scanning electron microscope (SEM). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images and energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping were recorded with a Tecnai G2 F20 UTWIN. Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5406 Å) was used to generate the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the samples on the Rigaku D/max-2500B2+/PC X-ray powder diffractometer. Raman spectra were measured by 532 nm laser excitation on a JY HR800. The Brunauer–Emmet–Teller (BET) surface area was obtained by isothermal nitrogen adsorption/desorption measurements on an ASAP 2020 (Micromeritics). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) using Al Kα radiation (1486.6 eV) was performed on an ESCALAB 250. (The electrodes used for element content analyses were not soaked in electrolytes, and the electrodes used for configuration analyses were separated from the assembled batteries.) For the depth test, to remove the surface, the samples were sputtered with an Ar<sup>+</sup> beam for 180 s at a voltage of 3 KeV and a current density of 75 μA cm<sup>–2</sup> before the XPS test. Soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) was performed in the Beijing synchrotron radiation facility with a step length of 0.05 eV.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The coin cells (CR2025) were assembled in a glovebox filled with argon (O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt;1 ppm). NP-CNFs-<i>x</i> and N-CNFs films were cut into 12 mm diameter disks that can be directly used as a working electrode. The separator used was glass fibers, and the electrolyte was 1.0 M NaSO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub>, which was dissolved in diglyme. For half cells, metallic sodium was used as the counter electrode. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) tests were performed in the voltage range of 0.01–2.8 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) on a LAND CT2001. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) was tested in the same voltage range on a CHI660E electrochemical workstation, and the scanning rate was 0.1 mV·s<sup>–1</sup>. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was tested in the frequency range of 0.01 Hz to 1 MHz on the CHI660E, and the sine wave amplitude was 5 mV. The pulse current and the pulse time for the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and 20 min, respectively, and the relaxation time was 2 h. For full batteries, the cathode was prepared by slurry coating. First, NVP, acetylene black, and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) at a weight ratio of 8:1:1 were mixed and ground into a slurry. Then, the slurry was coated on Al foil with a thickness of 150 μm and then dried in an oven at 120 °C for 12 h. Finally, NVP-coated Al foils were cut into disks as a cathode. The electrolyte used was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC), dimethyl carbonate (DMC), and methyl ethyl carbonate (EMC) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) (EC/DMC/EMC = 1:1:1 by volume). The voltage range of the galvanostatic discharge/charge test for full cells was 0–4.2 V.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1.  Preparation and Structural Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b, the prepared NP-CNFs-3.0 membrane with a size of 15 mm × 30 mm can be bent freely 180° without breaking, showing preeminent flexibility. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c,d) show that NP-CNFs-3.0 retains the three-dimensional (3D) interwoven network structure of its precursor (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, Supporting Information), which provides an efficient conductive path for electron transfer and maintains the structural integrity of carbon electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> It can be found that the diameter of carbon nanofibers gradually thickens with the introduction of APP (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, Supporting Information) because APP improves the viscosity of the spinning solution. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e) presents the typical disordered structure of HCs in the NP-CNFs-3.0. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> With the increase of APP, the carbon layer spacings are gradually expanded due to the introduction of large-radius P atoms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with the 3.58 Å of N-CNFs, the interlayer spacing of NP-CNFs-3.0 increased to ca. 3.92 Å, which facilitates the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref23" aria-label="reference 6 and 23">(6,23)</a> A high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscope (HAADF-STEM) was used to analyze the uniformity of doping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The homogeneous distribution of N, O, and P heteroatoms along the carbon nanofibers indicates that N and P are successfully doped into the carbon matrix.</div><div class="NLM_p">The crystal structure of carbon nanofibers was investigated by X-ray diffraction (XRD). The broad diffraction peak of the (002) plane appears at around 23° in the N-CNFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). With the introduction of APP, the (002) peak shifts to a lower angle bit by bit, indicating the gradual increase of the disordered degree of carbon texture. According to the Bragg equation, the (002) interlayer spacing is calculated to be 3.61 Å for N-CNFs, and it significantly increases to 3.87 Å for NP-CNFs-3.0 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, Supporting Information), which is consistent with the HRTEM analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, Supporting Information). Raman spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b) also confirms the increase of carbon layer disorder with the doping of P heteroatoms due to the increasing intensity ratio of the D-band and the G-band. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Surface or pore Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions are interference, which can cause Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms similar to heteroatoms. However, this interference could be avoided by doping with APP because the surface area and pore volume of NP-CNFs-3.0 are decreased. As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, all three samples show similar type IV isotherms in the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption curves. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> The specific surface area of NP-CNFs-1.0 (12.98 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) calculated by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method is almost the same as that of N-CNFs (12.41 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), while that of NP-CNFs-3.0 is only 7.48 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, density functional theoretical (DFT) pore size distributions exhibit similar micropore and mesopore structures in all three samples. Among them, NP-CNFs-3.0 has a minimum pore volume of only 0.014 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, which indicates that unlike H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, APP has little activation effect on the carbon matrix. The decreased surface area and pore volume could reduce the Na storage capacity contributed by surface or pore adsorption, which is helpful to reveal the effects of N and P on the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of carbon nanofibers.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/medium/am2c17789_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns; (b) Raman spectra; (c) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and pore size distributions; high-resolution N 1s spectra (d) on the surface and (e) in the bulk phase (after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching for 180 s) of NP-CNFs-3.0 and N-CNFs; (f) high-resolution P 2p spectra on the surface and bulk phase of NP-CNFs-3.0.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c17789&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.  Surface and Bulk Configurations of N and P Heteroatoms</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The elemental compositions and configurations on the surface and in the bulk phase of as-prepared N-CNFs and NP-CNFs-<i>x</i> are investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra. The surface elements of NP-CNFs-<i>x</i> are composed of C, N, P, and O (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, Supporting Information), which is consistent with elemental mapping measurements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f). The heteroatom contents are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. The N and P contents on the surface of NP-CNFs-3.0 are ca. 14.58 and 13.59 atom %, respectively, which are extraordinarily prominent compared with heteroatom-doped carbon materials in other literature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, Supporting Information). The surface N content of NP-CNFs-3.0 is obviously higher than that of N-CNFs (9.44 atom %), indicating that both N and P in APP participate in doping. The elemental composition in the bulk phase is further investigated by Ar<sup>+</sup> etching for 180 s. It can be found that heteroatomic contents in the bulk of NP-CNFs-3.0 are lower than those on the surface, indicating that doping tends to be more efficient on the surface. The N content in the bulk phase of NP-CNFs-3.0 decreases to 11.86 atom %, which is still higher than 10.6 atom % in N-CNFs. And the P content in the bulk phase of NP-CNFs-3.0 decreases to 11.7 atom %, which is also prominent compared with the results reported in the literature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, Supporting Information). More interestingly, the configurations of heteroatoms in the bulk phase of N-CNFs and NP-CNFs-3.0 are also different from those on the surface. The high-resolution N 1s spectra on the surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6a</a>, Supporting Information) could be deconvoluted into four peaks corresponding to N-6 (pyridine nitrogen, 398.1 eV), N-5 (pyrrole nitrogen, 399.2 eV), N-Q (quaternary nitrogen, 400.7 eV), and N–O (oxidized nitrogen, 403.8 eV). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a> On the surface of N-CNFs, N elements are mainly composed of a large amount of N-6 and N-Q and a small amount of N-5. In contrast, on the surface of NP-CNFs-3.0, the N-5 content significantly increases, while the N-Q decreases dramatically. Therefore, the introduction of P atoms promotes the formation of N-5, which is consistent with the previous report. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> After etching, N-5 in both N-CNFs and NP-CNFs-3.0 are increased, while the content of N-6 and N-Q obviously decreases (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e). Especially, the N-5 content in the bulk of NP-CNFs-3.0 reaches 56%, while N-Q nearly disappeared. On the surface of NP-CNFs-3.0, P mainly exists in the form of an oxidation state. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6b</a>, Supporting Information, the P 2p spectra could be deconvoluted to oxidized PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> functional groups including C–O–PO<sub>3</sub> (134.0 eV) and C<i><sub>x</sub></i>–P–O<i><sub>y</sub></i> configurations (133.0 eV, containing C–P–O<sub>3</sub>, C<sub>2</sub>–P–O<sub>2</sub>, and C<sub>3</sub>–P–O). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref30" aria-label="reference 12 and 30">(12,30)</a> This should be attributed to the fact that the electron-donating characteristic of P makes it oxygen-sensitive and easy to be oxidized to form PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> After etching, a new peak appears at 130.6 eV, which corresponds to the P–C bond (including C–P–C and P–C<sub>3</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 23, 32 and 33">(23,32,33)</a> In general, the unoxidized P–C bond is prone to be ignored because it is only exposed after enough etching, which may affect the clarification of Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of P-doped carbon.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.3.  Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">Half-cells were assembled to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of N-CNFs and NP-CNFs-<i>x</i>. As shown in the cyclic voltammograms (CV) curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>, Supporting Information), there are irreversible cathodic peaks between 0.1 and 0.7 V in the first cycle in all samples, which are attributed to the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of SEI layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> A pair of sharp redox peaks at 0.01–0.1 V can also be observed in N-CNFs, which is caused by the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> in the graphite layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> In the voltage region higher than redox peaks, CV curves show capacitive features, which is attributed to the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption process on the surface functional groups. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref34" aria-label="reference 23 and 34">(23,34)</a> With the addition of APP, the redox peaks at 0.01–0.1 V weaken until disappearing in the CV curves of NP-CNFs-3.0. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) measurements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>, Supporting Information) show that the specific capacity increases gradually with the APP addition (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>, Supporting Information). Among them, NP-CNFs-3.0 exhibits the highest reversible capacity of 329.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an initial coulomb efficiency (ICE) of 70.4% at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, while N-CNFs could only deliver 183.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an ICE of 65.3%. The higher ICE of NP-CNFs-3.0 should be attributed to its lower surface area, which reduces the surface side reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> It is worth noting that the capacity contribution of the plateau region decreases until disappears, and the increase in total capacity comes from the slope contribution. These results indicate that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior changes from the “adsorption-intercalation” model of N-CNFs to the “sole adsorption” one in NP-CNFs-3.0. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 14, 35 and 36">(14,35,36)</a> The transformation of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism could be caused by Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on surface or heteroatoms; however, it is hard for reduced surface area to contribute more adsorption capacity for NP-CNFs-3.0. Thus, this should be attributed to the increasing of heteroatoms, especially the introduction of P, which can expand the interlayer spacing and increase active sites in the carbon matrix. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref37" aria-label="reference 22 and 37">(22,37)</a> Furthermore, the disappearance of the plateau region at low voltage helps to avoid the safety issues of Na dendrite growth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 22, 38 and 39">(22,38,39)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/medium/am2c17789_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Na<sup>+</sup> storage performances of NP-CNFs-3.0 in half and full cells: (a) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) second circle GCD curves at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate performance; (d) comparison of rate performance of NP-CNFs-3.0 with N, P codoped hierarchical porous carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> N, P codoped hollow microporous carbon spheres, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> P-doped carbon nanofibers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> P-doped carbon nanosheets, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> N-doped carbon nanofibers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> S, P codoped hard carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> and P-functionalized hard carbon; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> (e) cycle performance of NP-CNFs-3.0 at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (f) GCD curves, (g) rate performance and (h) cycle performance of NP-CNFs-3.0 in full cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c17789&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">NP-CNFs-3.0 exhibits the highest specific capacity, superior rate, and cycle performance among all of the samples. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c shows the excellent rate performance of NP-CNFs-3.0, which delivers average reversible capacities of 329.0, 305.0, 285.7, 257.6, 234.3, 204.5, and 152.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. N-CNFs only provide 183.2, 173.6, 164.9, 152.3, 136.8, 108.8, and 72.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the same current densities. The high-rate cyclic stability of the samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, Supporting Information) is also enhanced with the addition of APP. Even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, NP-CNFs-3.0 exhibits a striking capacity of 125.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and retains 84% after 3000 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). Additionally, compared with other heteroatom-doped carbon materials in the literature, the electrochemical performance of NP-CNFs-3.0 is still remarkable (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>, Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that NP-CNFs-1.0 has higher N content (18.2 atom %) and specific surface area than NP-CNFs-3.0 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, Supporting Information), but its electrochemical performance is inferior to NP-CNFs-3.0, which could be because of the lower P content (9.88 atom %) in NP-CNFs-1.0. This suggests that P-species could yield a more pronounced influence on Na<sup>+</sup> storage, compared with N.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical kinetics was investigated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). On the one hand, EIS measurements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9 and S10, and Table S7</a>, Supporting Information) show that the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of NP-CNFs-3.0 (7.3 Ω) is less than a tenth of the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of N-CNFs (83.6 Ω), indicating the enhanced charge transfer kinetics of NP-CNFs-3.0. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> On the other hand, GITT measurements show that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) of NP-CNFs-3.0 (10<sup>–8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) is an order of magnitude higher than that of N-CNFs (10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) during the discharge/charge process, indicating the enhancement of the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S11 and S12</a>, Supporting Information). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> Additionally, it is worth noting that the D<sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of N-CNFs has an evident downward trend below 0.1 V, which could be attributed to the poor diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> in the plateau region. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> On the contrary, NP-CNFs-3.0 does not show a downward trend below 0.1 V due to the disappearance of the plateau region. Thus, N, P dual-doping improves the electron transfer and the diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>, which means that the length of the reaction route could be improved and Na<sup>+</sup> is able to diffuse into the deep part of NP-CNFs-3.0. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Full cells were assembled using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as cathode materials to examine the application potential of NP-CNFs-3.0. The electrochemical performance of NVP is consistent with the reference (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, Supporting Information). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f presents the GCD curves of the NP-CNFs-3.0//NVP full cell in the voltage range from 0 to 4.2 V at 0.15 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Based on the weight of the NP-CNFs-3.0 electrode, the reversible capacity is 215.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an ICE of 89.9% at 0.15 A g<sup>–1</sup>. At 0.3, 0.6, 1.5, 3, and 6 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the NP-CNFs-3.0//NVP full cell delivers an average capacity of 201.8, 181.1, 156.3, 133.5, and 112.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g). When the current density is switched back to 0.15 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity returns to 205.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, showing excellent rate performance. The full battery still maintains a specific capacity of 164.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h), corresponding to a capacity retention of 76%. The excellent electrochemical performance of NP-CNFs-3.0//NVP full batteries proves the potential application value of NP-CNFs-3.0 as an anode material for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.4.  Surface and Bulk Sodiation/Desodiation Mechanism</h3><div id="sec3_4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.4.1.  Surface Reactions</h4><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the roots of enhanced performances of NP-CNFs-3.0, the roles of surface and bulk doping on the electrochemical reactions were investigated. The surface reactions during the first cycle were detected by ex situ XPS and soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) based on the surface-sensitive total electron yield (TEY) mode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> XPS can distinguish the surface reactions of various N and P configurations. During the sodiation/desodiation process, there is little change in the binding energy of surface-oxidized P-functional groups (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b). This result is consistent with the those obtained in the previous report, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> indicating that the fully oxidized PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups is redox-inactive. Although the surface PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> does not contribute to the higher capacity by redox, it can enlarge the interlayer spacing and introduce more defective structures to enhance capacity as confirmed by XRD and Raman spectra <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> and help to avoid ‘trap effect’ caused by excessive heteroatoms, thus increasing ICE. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Furthermore, the inactive PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups could significantly promote the stability of electrochemical interface on NP-CNFs-3.0 by forming the C–P–O bridge between C and O atoms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> In the ex situ C K-edge sXAS spectra (TEY mode) of N-CNFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g), the peak at 290.5 eV, attributed to C 1s → π* transitions (C 2p–O 2p-derived orbitals) of the inorganic Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, could be observed at the pristine state, which is consistent with previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 47 and 50 to 54">(47,50−54)</a> It indicates that Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> formed by the decomposition of ether-based electrolyte generates immediately after the soak. And the charge/discharge reaction could remove the surface Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and produce an organic compound on the surface, which could be confirmed by the XPS spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>, Supporting Information). Thus, the violent change of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface of N-CNFs reveals its unstable surface. On the contrary, there is almost no Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> formation on the surface of NP-CNF-3.0 and its carbon matrix is relatively stable during sodiation/desodiation compared with N-CNFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S14b</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, surface PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> is inert, but it could inhibit the formation and decomposition of inorganic Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, which is always associated with poorer electrochemical performances <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> and thus stabilizes the electrochemical interface, which is the root of excellent cycle stability of NP-CNFs-3.0.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/medium/am2c17789_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Evolution of chemical states and electronic structures of elements at various potentials during (a) the first cycle charge/discharge curve of NP-CNFs-3.0 at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (b) surface deconvolved P 2p and (c) N 1s XPS spectra of NP-CNFs-3.0; (d) bulk phase deconvolved P 2p and (e) N 1s XPS spectra of NP-CNFs-3.0; normalized C K-edge sXAS spectra of (f) NP-CNFs-3.0 and (g) N-CNFs based on TEY mode; (h) normalized P L-edge and (i) N K-edge sXAS spectra of NP-CNFs-3.0 based on TFY mode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c17789&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">In addition, the activity of surface N-species is also changed by doped PO<i><sub>x</sub></i>. As for N-CNFs, N-Q and N-5 are involved in the surface process while N-6 is inactive, and the binding energies of its surface N-species move to low energies during discharging (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, on the surface of NP-CNFs-3.0, N-Q and N-5 are inactive, while N-6 becomes active (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). Nearly all of the N-6 configuration participates in the surface reaction at 0.01V, and its binding energy becomes higher, which is completely opposite to that of N-CNFs. Thus, on the surface, P-doping makes N-5 and N-Q more stable but promotes the activity of N-6.</div></div><div id="sec3_4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.4.2.  Bulk Reactions</h4><div class="NLM_p">In the bulk phase, the sodiation/desodiation processes were revealed by ex situ XPS after removing the surface layer by 180 s of Ar<sup>+</sup> depth etching and sXAS based on bulk-sensitive total fluorescence yield (TFY) mode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> As shown in the P 2p XPS spectra of NP-CNFs-3.0 during cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). The binding energies of C–O–PO<sub>3</sub>, C<i><sub>x</sub></i>–P–O<i><sub>y</sub></i>, and P–C all move to higher energies during sodiation, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> adsorbs on P-containing bonds. In the deep discharge state, all P-configurations shift to high binding energy sites (P–C/Na: ca. 132.8 eV; C–P–O/Na: ca. 133.6 eV; C–O–PO<sub>3</sub>/Na: ca. 134.8 eV), showing excellent activity. Among them, the change in the P–C bond binding energy is the most visible, indicating that its electrochemical activity is the highest, which is consistent with the previous studies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref40" aria-label="reference 24 and 40">(24,40)</a> During charging, C<i><sub>x</sub></i>–P–O<i><sub>y</sub></i> and P–C configurations first start to desodiate. At 2.8 V, all P-species return to their original binding energy, indicating a fully reversible sodiation process. The prominent reactivity and reversibility of P-species enable NP-CNFs-3.0 to achieve high rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The P L-edge sXAS spectra (TFY mode) of NP-CNFs-3.0 could also confirm the reactivity of P-species (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h). During discharging, the peak at 137.1 eV (caused by the 1s to π* transition of P═C or P═O) dramatically decreases, this characteristic peak shows a voltage-dependent change, indicating that P═C and P═O structures participate in the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> actively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> In addition, the peak caused by the 2s to 3p transition at around 141 eV (related to some sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized P) becomes sharp, and the position of the edge moves slightly to higher energy, which implies that P participates in the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> in the form of electron donor. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 56 and 57">(56,57)</a> Note that these dipole-forbidden 3p orbitals are usually mixed with the characters of O, and thus their transition is possible. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> After charging, the curve completely restores, suggesting that the sodiation process is totally reversible.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Bulk N-containing bonds also become active and reversible after P doping. As shown in the N 1s spectra of N-CNFs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>, Supporting Information), N has few contributions to the Na<sup>+</sup> storage, only a small amount of N-Q (about 28.7% of total N-Q species) takes part in the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> in the bulk phase, and it cannot completely restore after cycling. However, after doping, the activity of N-species in NP-CNFs-3.0 is promoted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e) and more N-species, including ca. 27.4% of N-5 and ca. 19.5% of N-6, participate in the sodiation reaction, but their activity is still weaker than P, which shows complete activity. In addition, all of the N-configurations of NP-CNFs-3.0 restore to the initial state after the first cycle, showing excellent electrochemical reversibility. The weak activity of N could also be observed in the N K-edge sXAS spectra based on TFY mode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S17</a>, Supporting Information). There is little change during the process of charge/discharge in both N-CNFs and NP-CNFs-3.0, which also indicates that the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption property of N-species is much lower than that of P.</div></div><div id="sec3_4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">3.4.3.  Sodiation/Desodiation Mechanism</h4><div class="NLM_p">For the sodium storage mechanism of N and P with different configurations and their synergistic effects, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>, the reactivities of various N-species with Na<sup>+</sup> on the surface and in the bulk phase are completely changed by P-doping. On the surface, the activity of N-5 and N-Q are inhibited while N-6 is promoted. In the bulk phase, N-Q is inhibited while N-5 and N-6 are promoted. Both on the surface and in the bulk phase, P-doping can inhibit the activity of N-Q and promote the activity of N-6.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/medium/am2c17789_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Reaction model of N, P configurations with Na<sup>+</sup> on the surface and in the bulk phase.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/asset/images/large/am2c17789_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c17789&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In view of the above phenomena, we propose that P-doping can promote the electron-donating ability of N-configurations and suppress their electron absorption characteristics. Based on the change in the XPS of N-CNFs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S15 and S16</a>, Supporting Information), the binding energies of N-Q and N-5 on the surface and N-Q in the bulk phase (all other configurations are inactive) shift to lower energies during Na<sup>+</sup> storage, showing electron absorption characteristics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> After P-doping, the original electron-withdrawing configurations (the above three species) become inactive. However, for the original inactive configurations (N-6 on the surface and N-5 and N-6 in the bulk phase), their binding energies shift to a higher direction in the process of Na<sup>+</sup> storage, showing the characteristics of electron donors. This indicates that the N-configurations with electron absorption ability are inhibited from becoming inactive configurations, while the inactive configurations are promoted to become electron donors.</div><div class="NLM_p">In the case of P, all oxidized P groups are inactive on the surface, while all P-configurations including oxidized P groups and unoxidized P–C are active in the bulk phase. The distinct activity of PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> should be attributed to the separate chemical environment on the surface and in the bulk phase. This can also be reflected by the Na 1s XPS spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>, Supporting Information). Both on the surface and in the bulk of N-CNFs, a trifling change in Na binding energy could be observed, and the Na 1s peak presents at 1071.0 eV on the surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the predominance of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> which is consistent with the sXAS analysis results. After peeling off the surface layer, the binding energy of Na in the bulk of N-CNFs slightly shifts to 1070.8 eV, suggesting Na is stored as the intercalated state (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18c</a>, Supporting Information), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> and it changes slightly during cycling, showing a weak sodiation reaction.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Compared with N-CNFs, the main peak of Na 1s spectra on the surface of NP-CNFs-3.0 appears at 1070.5 eV, indicating that inorganic Na mainly exists in the form of the Na<sub>2</sub>O/NaF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18b</a>, Supporting Information). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> These results also confirm that PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> could inhibit the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. Compared with Na<sub>2</sub>O/NaF, high Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> content on the surface could be harmful to electrochemical stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> As reported, phosphate additives in electrolytes could effectively promote the stability of SEI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 59 and 60">(59,60)</a> The surface PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups could play a similar role. In addition, compared with the surface, the binding energy of Na in the bulk phase of NP-CNFs-3.0 shifts to 1072.0 eV at the pristine state (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c17789/suppl_file/am2c17789_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18d</a>, Supporting Information). The huge difference in Na binding energy between the surface and bulk of NP-CNFs-3.0 reflects that Na is in completely different chemical environments. The binding energy of Na obviously decreases to 1071.5 eV during discharging and then reversibly restores to 1072.0 eV after charging. The changing trend of the Na binding energy is opposite to that of P-configurations during cycling. This presents the reversible electron transfer between Na and P-configurations, which should be attributed to the electron-rich bulk phase of NP-CNFs-3.0. Noticeably, the Na binding energy of NP-CNFs-3.0 is aloof from the interacted state, indicating that Na is stored in the adsorption state, which is consistent with CV curves. Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption activity of bulk P-configurations is the root of the transition of the Na storage mechanism from “absorption intercalation” to the “sole absorption” model. The feature of a stable electrochemical interface and an active bulk phase is significantly important to achieve high capacity and stable cycle performance for SIBs.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, NP-CNFs with ultrahigh N and P contents and low specific surface area are prepared by electrospinning and in situ pyrolysis, using PAN and APP as sources. By employing NP-CNFs as a platform, the different configurations of N and P as well as their influence on the Na<sup>+</sup> storage are clarified. In both surface and the bulk phase, P-doping affects Na<sup>+</sup> storage more significantly compared with N. On the surface, all P-configurations belonging to oxidized PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> groups, including C–O–PO<sub>3</sub> and C<i><sub>x</sub></i>–P–O<i><sub>y</sub></i>, are inactive for Na<sup>+</sup> storage but enhance the stability of electrochemical interface. The unoxidized P–C configuration also presents except the PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> in the bulk phase where both P–C and PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> represent fabulous Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption activity and complete reversibility. In addition, P-doping changes the Na<sup>+</sup> storage activity and reversibility of various N-configurations both on the surface and in the bulk due to the modification of P on the electron-donating ability of N. Moreover, with the increase of P-doping content, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage model of carbon nanofibers gradually changes from “absorption intercalation” to the “sole absorption” one, and the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity and ion-diffusion kinetics are also markedly promoted. Significantly, NP-CNFs-3.0 in half cells delivers a high capacity of 329.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> and retains 152.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. And in full cells, the NP-CNFs-3.0 also exhibits excellent rate performance and cyclic stability. This work provides a new strategy for the development of high-level N, P dual-doped hard carbon and suggests a new insight into the effects of P-configurations with both surface inertness and bulk activity on Na<sup>+</sup> storage.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i17">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c17789?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c17789</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM image of NP-CNFs-3.0 precursor; SEM images and diameter distributions of NP-CNFs-3.0, NP-CNFs-2.0, NP-CNFs-1.0, NP-CNFs-0.5, and N-CNFs; HRTEM images and carbon layer distance measurements of NP-CNFs-3.0, NP-CNFs-1.0, and N-CNFs; TEM, STEM, and elemental mapping images of NP-CNFs-1.0; XPS survey spectra on surface and bulk of NP-CNFs-3.0, NP-CNFs-1.0, and N-CNFs; high-resolution N 1s and P 2p spectra of NP-CNFs-1.0.                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Nanosheet Frameworks Derived from Peat Moss as High Performance Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Wang, Huanlei; Li, Zhi; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Cui, Kai; Xu, Zhanwei; Zahiri, Beniamin; Tan, Xuehai; Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Olsen, Brian C.; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11004-11015</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Peat moss, a wild plant that covers 3% of the earth's surface, serves as an ideal precursor to create Na ion battery (NIB) anodes with some of the most attractive electrochem. properties ever reported for carbonaceous materials.  By inheriting the unique cellular structure of peat moss leaves, the resultant materials are composed of 3-dimensional macroporous interconnected networks of C nanosheets (as thin as 60 nm).  The peat moss tissue is highly cross-linked, being rich in lignin and hemicellulose, suppressing the nucleation of equil. graphite even at 1100°.  Rather, the carbons form highly ordered pseudographitic arrays with substantially larger intergraphene spacing (0.388 nm) than graphite (c/2 = 0.3354 nm).  XRD anal. demonstrates that this allows for significant Na intercalation to occur even &lt;0.2 V vs. Na/Na+.  By also incorporating a mild (300°) air activation step, the authors introduce hierarchical micro- and mesoporosity that tremendously improves the high rate performance through facile electrolyte access and further reduced Na ion diffusion distances.  The optimized structures (carbonization at 1100° + activation) result in a stable cycling capacity of 298 mA-h g-1 (after 10 cycles, 50 mA g-1), with ∼150 mA-h g-1 of charge accumulating between 0.1 and 0.001 V with negligible voltage hysteresis in that region, nearly 100% cycling Coulombic efficiency, and superb cycling retention and high rate capacity (255 mA-h g-1 at the 210th cycle, stable capacity of 203 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9g5dheVSGj7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li6gwCiC9DbWg"> &gt;&gt; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrolyte-Dependent Sodium Ion Transport Behaviors in Hard Carbon Anode</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>16</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">2001053</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/smll.202001053</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D16%26publication_year%3D2020%26journal%3DSmall%26author%3DM.%2BE.%2BLee%26author%3DS.%2BM.%2BLee%26author%3DJ.%2BChoi%26author%3DD.%2BJang%26author%3DS.%2BLee%26author%3DH.-J.%2BJin%26author%3DY.%2BS.%2BYun%26title%3DElectrolyte-Dependent%2BSodium%2BIon%2BTransport%2BBehaviors%2Bin%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnode%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fsmll.202001053&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c17789&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fsmll.202001053&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrolyte-Dependent Sodium Ion Transport Behaviors in Hard Carbon Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Min Eui; Lee, Sang Moon; Choi, Jaewon; Jang, Dawon; Lee, Sungho; Jin, Hyoung-Joon; Yun, Young Soo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">35</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2001053</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive study is conducted on hard carbon (HC) series samples by tuning the graphitic local microstructures systematically as an anode for SIBs in both carbonate- (CBE) and glyme-based electrolytes (GBE).  The results reveal more detailed charge storage characters of HCs on the LVP section. 1 The LVP capacity is closely related to the prismatic surface area to the basal plane as well as the bulk d., regardless of electrolyte systems. 2 The glyme-sodium ion complex can facilitate sodium ion delivery into the internal closed pores of the HCs along with not well-ordered graphitic structures. 3 The glyme-mediated sodium ion-storage behavior causes significant decreases in both surface film resistance and charge transfer resistance, leading to enhanced rate capability. 4 The LVP originates from the formation of pseudo-metallic sodium nanoclusters, which are the same in a CBE and GBE.  These results provide insight into the sodium ion-storage behaviors of HCs, particularly on the interrelationship between graphitic local microstructures and electrolyte systems.  In addn., a high-performance HC anode with a plateau capacity of ≈300 mA h g-1 is designed based on the information, and its workability is demonstrated in a full-cell SIB device.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrmn9IjZjUxjbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgLtmGlG1gADw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFGiurvO&amp;md5=c41ca45b344029cc642cb5324c0a3d0c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201602898"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Anode Materials for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Heteroatom Doping: An Effective Way to Boost Sodium Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yu; Chen, Minghua; Liu, Bo; Zhang, Yan; Liang, Xinqi; Xia, Xinhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">27</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000927</span>CODEN:
