                        cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard carbon for sodium storage: mechanism and optimization strategies toward commercialization</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Dequan; Zhang, Wen; Luo, Kangying; Song, Yang; Zhong, Yanjun; Liu, Yuxia; Wang, Gongke; Zhong, Benhe; Wu, Zhenguo; Guo, Xiaodong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2244-2262</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have shown promising prospects for complementarity to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the field of grid-scale energy storage.  After a decade of continuous fundamental research on SIBs, it's becoming increasingly urgent to advance the commercialization.  For SIB anode materials, hard carbon is the most mature and currently the only material likely to be commercialized, but it is still far away from large-scale industrialization.  Herein, we carry out a comprehensive overview of the current state of the art in terms of three main aspects.  Firstly, a fundamental understanding of the microstructure and sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon is introduced, which can be categorized into three different processes: capacitive adsorption, nanopore filling, and intercalation in carbon interlayers.  Then, based on an in-depth understanding of the sodium storage mechanism, optimization methods in terms of increasing the specific capacity, rate performance, initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), and long-cycling stability are comprehensively summarized and analyzed.  Finally, potential methods and assocd. benefits for the design of carbon structures and the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) are discussed, hoping to provide useful guidelines for future research and commercialization.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo4e0jCHDDXgrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhsRKlPpFIs5w"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling Fast Na+ Transfer Kinetics in the Whole-Voltage-Region of Hard-Carbon Anodes for Ultrahigh-Rate Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yin, Xiuping; Lu, Zhixiu; Wang, Jing; Feng, Xiaochen; Roy, Swagata; Liu, Xiangsi; Yang, Yong; Zhao, Yufeng; Zhang, Jiujun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Efficient electrode materials, that combine high power and high energy, are the crucial requisites of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which have unwrapped new possibilities in the areas of grid-scale energy storage.  Hard carbons (HCs) are considered as the leading candidate anode materials for SIBs, however, the primary challenge of slow charge-transfer kinetics at the low potential region (&lt;0.1 V) remains unresolved till date, and the underlying structure-performance correlation is under debate.  Herein, ultrafast sodium storage in the whole-voltage-region (0.01-2 V), with the Na+ diffusion coeff. enhanced by 2 orders of magnitude (≈10-7 cm2 s-1) through rationally deploying the phys. parameters of HCs using a ZnO-assisted bulk etching strategy is reported.  It is unveiled that the Na+ adsorption energy (Ea) and diffusion barrier (Eb) are in a pos. and neg. linear relationship with the carbon p-band center, resp., and balance of Ea and Eb is crit. in enhancing the charge-storage kinetics.  The charge-storage mechanism in HCs is evidenced through comprehensive in(ex) situ techniques.  The as prepd. HCs microspheres deliver a record high rate performance of 107 mAh g-1 @ 50 A g-1 and unprecedented electrochem. performance at extremely low temp. (426 mAh g-1 @ -40°C).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpZUKSxfBJiLrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj8tjt7RlDA3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XkvFShsLc%253D&amp;md5=865898bc8fbf211a547b34c3e49e9f46</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202104167"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding and Calibration of Charge Storage Mechanism in Cyclic Voltammetry Curves</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Noticeable pseudo-capacitance behavior out of charge storage mechanism (CSM) has attracted intensive studies because it can provide both high energy d. and large output power.  Although cyclic voltammetry is recognized as the feasible electrochem. technique to det. it quant. in the previous works, the results are inferior due to uncertainty in the definitions and application conditions.  Herein, three successive treatments, including de-polarization, de-residual and de-background, as well as a nonlinear fitting algorithm are employed for the 1st time to calibrate the different CSM contribution of three typical cathode materials, LiFePO4, LiMn2O4 and Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7, and achieve well-sepd. phys. capacitance, pseudo-capacitance and diffusive contributions to the total capacity.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Antiblocking Heterostructure to Accelerate Kinetic Process for Na-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Dianding; Liu, Kunhong; Hu, Junping; Zhou, Jisheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Heterostructures are attracting increasing attention in the field of sodium-ion batteries.  However, it is still unclear whether any two monophase components can be used to construct a high-performance heterostructure for sodium-ion batteries, as well as the kind of heterostructures that can boost electrochem. performances.  In this study, based on classical semiconductor theories on antiblocking and blocking interfaces, attempts are made to answer the abovementioned queries.  For this purpose, NiTe2-ZnTe antiblocking and CoTe2-ZnTe blocking heterostructures are synthesized through a bimetal-hexamine framework-derived strategy.  The NiTe2-ZnTe antiblocking heterostructure exhibits excellent high-rate and cycling performances, while the CoTe2-ZnTe blocking heterostructure performs poorly, even compared to their monophase components.  Further, kinetic measurements and theor. calcn. confirm that antiblocking heterointerfaces can boost Na-ion diffusion efficiency and decrease the diffusion barrier, which can be attributed to the highly conductive antiblocking heterointerfaces generated due to electron transfer from NiTe2 to ZnTe.  Therefore, this study provides a new perspective to design heterostructures more efficiently, with significantly better Na-ion storage performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqiIwjOMNIvXbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj8tjt7RlDA3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhvFOitQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=e7a0b8b6ed407b13696661dfce1c6af1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700959"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bak, S.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Anasori, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gogotsi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Ion Intercalation and Charge Storage Mechanism in 2D Vanadium Carbide</span>. <i>Adv.                 10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.103937</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D64%26publication_year%3D2019%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DD.%2BSun%26author%3DB.%2BLuo%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BTang%26author%3DX.%2BJi%26author%3DL.%2BWang%26title%3DEngineering%2Bthe%2BTrap%2BEffect%2Bof%2BResidual%2BOxygen%2BAtoms%2Band%2BDefects%2Bin%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnode%2Btowards%2BHigh%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2019.103937&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c17789&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2019.103937&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Engineering the trap effect of residual oxygen atoms and defects in hard carbon anode towards high initial Coulombic efficiency</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Dan; Luo, Bin; Wang, Haiyan; Tang, Yougen; Ji, Xiaobo; Wang, Lianzhou</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">64</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">103937</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is a promising anode candidate for lithium/sodium ion batteries due to the key features of low operation potential and low cost, but its practical utilization is hindered by a challenging issue of poor initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which has not been understood well and resolved properly.  Herein, we report a new in-situ engineering approach to deliberately tune the residual oxygen atoms/defects of hard carbon by controlling the atm. of pyrolysis synthesis process and reveal important correlations between the ICE and residual oxygen atoms/defects.  When used as an anode in sodium ion battery, the hard carbon electrode with reduced residual oxygen atoms and defects can achieve a high av. ICE above 85%, which is considerably higher than the commonly obsd. ∼70% and ∼30% ICE values for pristine and acid treated hard carbon electrodes.  Encouragingly, a high reversible capacity of 310 mAh g-1 with good cycling stability (93% after 100 cycles) is demonstrated at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1.  The d. functional theory (DFT) calcn. and exptl. results reveal that the trap effects of residual oxygen atoms and defects on Na+ are the key factors that impact the ICE of the hard carbon electrode.  When the hard carbon is coupled with Na3V2(PO4)2F3 cathode to form a sodium ion full cell, the battery delivers an impressively high energy d. of 239 Wh/kg (based on the active mass of anode and cathode without additives), which is among the best performing sodium ion full cells.  This work not only provides an effective approach to engineer the heteroatoms and defects in carbon-based materials but also sheds light on the design principle of practical hard carbon electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqkEnUylIAeH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj8tjt7RlDA3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhsFGjt7rK&amp;md5=e120935160c920c765491f0ec33bcdc3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1149/1945-7111/ab75fd"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fondard, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Irisarri, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Courrèges, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palacin, M.                         cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SEI composition on hard carbon in Na-Ion batteries after long cycling: Influence of salts (NaPF6, NaTFSI) and Additives (FEC, DMCF)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fondard, J.; Irisarri, E.; Courreges, C.; Palacin, M.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A study of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on hard-carbon (HC) electrodes in sodium half-cells is presented.  Electrochem. performances over &gt; 100 cycles were compared with two different salts (NaPF6, NaTFSI) and two different electrolyte additives (FEC, DMCF) in a mixt. of EC and DMC solvents.  The best electrochem. performances were obsd. with NaPF6 salt in conjunction with 3% FEC.  The DMCF additive had a detrimental effect in all electrolyte combinations.  The chem. characterization of the SEI was carried out by XPS and showed that the best electrochem. behavior was related to an SEI compn. based on sodium ethylene dicarbonate and NaF, whereas poorer electrochem. performances were assocd. to either low NaF or high Na2CO3 content.  The results reported herein provide an insight on the SEI chem. on hard carbon electrodes in sodium cells after long-term cycling, as a complement to previous studies dealing with the first cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq4rxL0JhE-c7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj8tjt7RlDA3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXosVKisL0%253D&amp;md5=fc871aceb661bd6af19f8153b66817bd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1039/B313434B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Augustsson, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Herstedt, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Edström, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhuang, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">X-ray absorption and emission near the C 1s edge in graphite of anodes cycled in Li-ion batteries, were measured.  Resonant-excited emission spectra of the graphite anodes exhibit features characteristic of highly-oriented pyrolytic graphite as well as polycryst. graphite.  Spectra of 3 electrodes cycled in 2 different electrolytes are presented and compared with spectra of a pristine electrode.  A solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) was detected on the electrochem.-cycled electrodes.  Using selective excitation, resonant x-ray emission spectra of the SEI-species were obtained and compared to spectra of ref. compds.  The SEI on the cycled graphite anode consisted of lithium oxalate, Li succinate and Li methoxide.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpKrSzlen6fzrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgwK-8wpE2F7g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2cXmtlyrsLg%253D&amp;md5=883efbb3ee8ddd7598f89062a6a55451</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.2136/sssaj2008.0273"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gillespie, A.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Soil Science Society of America</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The rhizosphere is a region of complex interactions among plants, soil, and microbiota and by its very nature, it presents many tech. challenges to the analyst.  Whereas previous studies have generally focused on root exudation in artificial systems, we compared whole-soil samples from bulk and rhizosphere soils developed under field pea (Pisum sativa L.).  Synchrotron-based C and N K-edge x-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy and pyrolysis field-ionization mass-spectrometry (Py-FIMS) were used to investigate plant effects on the org. chem. of the rhizosphere in a growth chamber expt.  Soil type appeared to play a significant role in the development of the rhizosphere.  Indeed, the C and N K-edge XANES analyses revealed patterns of rhizosphere development that were strongly influenced by soil type.  Relative to the bulk soil, pea rhizosphere developed on a clay soil showed increases in heterocyclic N compds., proteins, and carboxylates.  Pea rhizospheres developed on a sandy clay loam soil showed increased nitroarom. compds. and reduced aroms., while proteins, carbohydrates, and carboxylic compds. remained unchanged relative to the bulk soils.  Pyrolysis mass spectrometry results showed that rhizosphere processes promote selective enrichment of lipid compds. (alkanes, alkenes, alkylamides, alkylaroms., and alkyl, benzoic, and phthalic esters) with a concomitant depletion of carbohydrates, proteins, phenols, and lignins.  These results suggest enhanced decompn. of labile org. matter at a mol. level in the rhizosphere, presumably as a result of enhanced microbial activity.  The presence of nitroarom. and alkylamide compds. in the rhizosphere indicates a unique cycling of N in this region.  Synchrotron-based anal. of whole soils offers useful, corroborating information regarding the org. chem. of the rhizosphere when compared with established pyrolytic techniques.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq-CpEHBq27NbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgwK-8wpE2F7g"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Analysis of the Stable Interphase Responsible for the Excellent Electrochemical Performance of Graphite Electrodes in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Zhaohua; Yang, Haoyi; Liu, Yiran; Bai, Ying; Chen, Guanghai; Li, Ying; Wang, Xinran; Xu, Huajie; Wu, Chuan; Lu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Considerable efforts have been exerted to understand the formation and properties of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) in sodium ion batteries.  However, the puzzling existence and role of SEI behind the huge vol. changes of the graphite electrodes need to be answered.  Herein, the reason of how ether-derived SEI maintains excellent reversibility despite the huge vol. changes during cycling is unraveled.  Theor. simulations and Fourier-transform IR spectroscopy demonstrate the formation mechanism of an SEI between the graphite anode and electrolyte.  Furthermore, the high mech. tolerance of the ether-derived SEI is confirmed in at. force microscopy.  A depth profile of XPS points to a multilayer structure of the ether-derived SEI.  The outer layer comprises orgs. (sodium alkoxide), while the inorgs. (Na2CO3, NaF) in interior region are mixed with some orgs.  Notably, the presence of orgs. ensures the adaptability of the SEI to the vol. expansion of graphite during cycling, and the concd. distribution of inorgs. improves the Young's modulus (resistance to deformation).  Therefore, the graphite anode exhibits high cycle stability (96.6% capacity retention ratio at 1 A g-1 over 860 cycles) and efficiency (≈99.5%).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo6VJ3B-G7caLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgwK-8wpE2F7g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXisVyis7%252FE&amp;md5=5ef7c802961382be4f256d016759fda7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.10.030"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yogi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takamatsu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamanaka, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Arai, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Uchimoto, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kojima, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ohta, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogumi, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Soft X-ray Absorption Spectroscopic Studies with Different Probing Depths: Effect of an Electrolyte Additive on Electrode Surfaces</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Soft X-ray absorption spectroscopic studies with different probing depths: Effect of an electrolyte additive on electrode surfaces</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yogi, Chihiro; Takamatsu, Daiko; Yamanaka, Keisuke; Arai, Hajime; Uchimoto, Yoshiharu; Kojima, Kazuo; Watanabe, Iwao; Ohta, Toshiaki; Ogumi, Zenpachi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">248</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed on a model LiCoO2 electrode was analyzed by the ultra-soft x-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS).  The data of Li K-, B K-, C K-, O K-, and Co L-edges spectra for the SEI film on the electrode were collected using three detection methods with different probing depths.  The electrode was prepd. by a pulsed laser deposition method.  All the spectral data consistently indicated that the SEI film contg. Li carbonate was instantly formed just after the soak of the electrode into the electrolyte soln. and that it decompd. during the repeated charge-discharge reactions.  The decompn. of the SEI film seems to cause the deterioration in Li ion battery cycle performance.  By adding Li bis(oxalate) borate (LiBOB) to the electrolyte the decompn. could be suppressed leading to longer cycle life.                         cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly durable organic electrode for sodium-ion batteries via a stabilized α-C radical intermediate</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Shaofei; Wang, Wenxi; Li, Minchan; Cao, Lujie; Lyu, Fucong; Yang, Mingyang; Wang, Zhenyu; Shi, Yang; Nan, Bo; Yu, Sicen; Sun, Zhifang; Liu, Yao; Lu, Zhouguang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is a challenge to prep. org. electrodes for sodium-ion batteries with long cycle life and high capacity.  The highly reactive radical intermediates generated during the sodiation/desodiation process could be a crit. issue because of undesired side reactions.  Here we present durable electrodes with a stabilized α-C radical intermediate.  Through the resonance effect as well as steric effects, the excessive reactivity of the unpaired electron is successfully suppressed, thus developing an electrode with stable cycling for over 2,000 cycles with 96.8% capacity retention.  In addn., the α-radical demonstrates reversible transformation between three states: C=C; α-C·radical; and α-C- anion.  Such transformation provides addnl. Na+ storage equal to more than 0.83 Na+ insertion per α-C radical for the electrodes.  The strategy of intermediate radical stabilization could be enlightening in the design of org. electrodes with enhanced cycling life and energy storage capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrxhHeCYpkzIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgwK-8wpE2F7g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvVSht73I&amp;md5=1fc3cc04ca28e6ceacd36a77f74da330</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1002/admi.202101773"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muñoz-Márquez, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable Li-ion battery technol. has progressed due to the development of a suitable combination of electroactive materials, binders, electrolytes, additives, and electrochem. cycling protocols that resulted in the formation of a stable electrode-electrolyte interphase.  It is expected that Na-ion technol. will attain a position comparable to Li-ion batteries dependent on advancements in establishing a stable electrode-electrolyte interphase.  However, Li and Na are both alkali metals with similar characteristics, yet the physicochem. properties of these systems differ.  For this reason, a detailed study on the electrode-electrolyte interphase properties, compn., and structure is required to understand the factors that influence the battery's behavior.                 10.1039/c2ee03445j</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2012%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DS.%2BYang%26author%3DD.%2BWang%26author%3DG.%2BLiang%26author%3DY.%2BM.%2BYiu%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BLiu%26author%3DX.%2BSun%26author%3DT.-K.%2BSham%26title%3DSoft%2BX-ray%2BXANES%2BStudies%2Bof%2BVarious%2BPhases%2BRelated%2Bto%2BLiFePO4%2BBased%2BCathode%2BMaterials%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc2ee03445j&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c17789&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc2ee03445j&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Soft X-ray XANES studies of various phases related to LiFePO4 based cathode materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Songlan; Wang, Dongniu; Liang, Guoxian; Yiu, Yun Miu; Wang, Jiajun; Liu, Lijia; Sun, Xueliang; Sham, Tsun-Kong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7007-7016</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">LiFePO4 has been a promising cathode material for rechargeable lithium ion batteries.  Different secondary or impurity phases, forming during either synthesis or subsequent redox process under normal operating conditions, can have a significant impact on the performance of the electrode.  The exploration of the electronic and chem. structures of impurity phases is crucial to understand such influence.  We have embarked on a series of synchrotron-based X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy studies for the element speciation in various impurity phase materials relevant to LiFePO4 for Li ion batteries.  In the present report, soft-X-ray XANES spectra of Li K-edge, P L2,3-edge, O K-edge and Fe L2,3-edge have been obtained for LiFePO4 in cryst., disordered and amorphous forms and some possible "impurities", including LiPO3, Li4P2O7, Li3PO4, Fe3(PO4)2, FePO4, and Fe2O3.  The results indicate that each element from different pure ref. compds. exhibits unique spectral features in terms of energy position, shape and intensity of the resonances in its XANES.  In addn., inverse partial fluorescence yield (IPFY) reveals the surface vs. bulk property of the specimens.  Therefore, the spectral data provided here can be used as stds. in the future for phase compn. anal.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo07UjH959I5bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liStDOQBo0EvQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XmsVWqsL4%253D&amp;md5=8cb86a65a8d783a244a068ac7b92b379</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit59" data-doi="10.1038/s41560-017-0033-8"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sodeyama, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takada, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tateyama, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fire-Extinguishing Organic Electrolytes for Safe Batteries</span>. <i>Nat. Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">22</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">29</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/s41560-017-0033-8</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D3%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D22-29%26journal%3DNat.%2BEnergy%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BYamada%26author%3DK.%2BSodeyama%26author%3DE.%2BWatanabe%26author%3DK.%2BTakada%26author%3DY.%2BTateyama%26author%3DA.%2BYamada%26title%3DFire-Extinguishing%2BOrganic%2BElectrolytes%2Bfor%2BSafe%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41560-017-0033-8&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c17789&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41560-017-0033-8&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fire-extinguishing organic electrolytes for safe batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jianhui; Yamada, Yuki; Sodeyama, Keitaro; Watanabe, Eriko; Takada, Koji; Tateyama, Yoshitaka; Yamada, Atsuo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">22-29</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2058-7546</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Severe safety concerns are impeding the large-scale employment of lithium/sodium batteries.  Conventional electrolytes are highly flammable and volatile, which may cause catastrophic fires or explosions.  Efforts to introduce flame-retardant solvents into the electrolytes have generally resulted in compromised battery performance because those solvents do not suitably passivate carbonaceous anodes.  Here we report a salt-concd. electrolyte design to resolve this dilemma via the spontaneous formation of a robust inorg. passivation film on the anode.  We demonstrate that a concd. electrolyte using a salt and a popular flame-retardant solvent (tri-Me phosphate), without any additives or soft binders, allows stable charge-discharge cycling of both hard-carbon and graphite anodes for more than 1,000 cycles (over one year) with negligible degrdn.; this performance is comparable or superior to that of conventional flammable carbonate electrolytes.  The unusual passivation character of the concd. electrolyte coupled with its fire-extinguishing property contributes to developing safe and long-lasting batteries, unlocking the limit toward development of much higher energy-d. batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqh5VD_9omOWrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liStDOQBo0EvQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXitVehtbc%253D&amp;md5=f17e94b071fcc675467c969b0b2e28ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit60" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA00724K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jang, J. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, K. T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Interfacial Architectures Based on a Binary Additive Combination for High-Performance Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> Anodes in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">8332</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">8338</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C5TA00724K</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D3%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D8332-8338%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BJang%26author%3DY.%2BLee%26author%3DY.%2BKim%26author%3DJ.%2BLee%26author%3DS.%2BLee%26author%3DK.%2BT.%2BLee%26author%3DN.%2BChoi%26title%3DInterfacial%2BArchitectures%2BBased%2Bon%2Ba%2BBinary%2BAdditive%2BCombination%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSn4P3%2BAnodes%2Bin%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC5TA00724K&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c17789&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC5TA00724K&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interfacial architectures based on a binary additive combination for high-performance Sn4P3 anodes in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jang, Jun Yeong; Lee, Yongwon; Kim, Youngjin; Lee, Jeongmin; Lee, Sang-Min; Lee, Kyu Tae; Choi, Nam-Soon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8332-8338</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors demonstrate the important strategy to design suitable electrolyte systems that make the desirable interfacial structure to allow the reversible sodiation/desodiation of Sn4P3 anodes.  The study reveals that the remarkable improvement in the electrochem. performance of Sn4P3 anodes for NIBs is achieved by the combination of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) with tris(trimethylsilyl)phosphite (TMSP).  The authors clearly present the unique functions of this binary additive combination to build up a protective surface film on the Sn4P3 anode against unwanted electrolyte decompn. and to prevent the formation of the Na15Sn4 phase, which is accompanied by a large vol. expansion during the Na insertion (sodiation) process.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr-GdkWIWK-XLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh1Bh3odtw5bw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjs1Orsbk%253D&amp;md5=ee283525171f4fdd8c7fd23fe8ac0e76</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script                         rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Nanosheet Frameworks Derived from Peat Moss as High Performance Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Wang, Huanlei; Li, Zhi; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Cui, Kai; Xu, Zhanwei; Zahiri, Beniamin; Tan, Xuehai; Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Olsen, Brian C.; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11004-11015</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Peat moss, a wild plant that covers 3% of the earth's surface, serves as an ideal precursor to create Na ion battery (NIB) anodes with some of the most attractive electrochem. properties ever reported for carbonaceous materials.  By inheriting the unique cellular structure of peat moss leaves, the resultant materials are composed of 3-dimensional macroporous interconnected networks of C nanosheets (as thin as 60 nm).  The peat moss tissue is highly cross-linked, being rich in lignin and hemicellulose, suppressing the nucleation of equil. graphite even at 1100°.  Rather, the carbons form highly ordered pseudographitic arrays with substantially larger intergraphene spacing (0.388 nm) than graphite (c/2 = 0.3354 nm).  XRD anal. demonstrates that this allows for significant Na intercalation to occur even &lt;0.2 V vs. Na/Na+.  By also incorporating a mild (300°) air activation step, the authors introduce hierarchical micro- and mesoporosity that tremendously improves the high rate performance through facile electrolyte access and further reduced Na ion diffusion distances.  The optimized structures (carbonization at 1100° + activation) result in a stable cycling capacity of 298 mA-h g-1 (after 10 cycles, 50 mA g-1), with ∼150 mA-h g-1 of charge accumulating between 0.1 and 0.001 V with negligible voltage hysteresis in that region, nearly 100% cycling Coulombic efficiency, and superb cycling retention and high rate capacity (255 mA-h g-1 at the 210th cycle, stable capacity of 203 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9g5dheVSGj7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lincjVdRn68Bg"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrolyte-Dependent Sodium Ion Transport Behaviors in Hard Carbon Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Min Eui; Lee, Sang Moon; Choi, Jaewon; Jang, Dawon; Lee, Sungho; Jin, Hyoung-Joon; Yun, Young Soo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive study is conducted on hard carbon (HC) series samples by tuning the graphitic local microstructures systematically as an anode for SIBs in both carbonate- (CBE) and glyme-based electrolytes (GBE).  The results reveal more detailed charge storage characters of HCs on the LVP section. 1 The LVP capacity is closely related to the prismatic surface area to the basal plane as well as the bulk d., regardless of electrolyte systems. 2 The glyme-sodium ion complex can facilitate sodium ion delivery into the internal closed pores of the HCs along with not well-ordered graphitic structures. 3 The glyme-mediated sodium ion-storage behavior causes significant decreases in both surface film resistance and charge transfer resistance, leading to enhanced rate capability. 4 The LVP originates from the formation of pseudo-metallic sodium nanoclusters, which are the same in a CBE and GBE.  These results provide insight into the sodium ion-storage behaviors of HCs, particularly on the interrelationship between graphitic local microstructures and electrolyte systems.  In addn., a high-performance HC anode with a plateau capacity of ≈300 mA h g-1 is designed based on the information, and its workability is demonstrated in a full-cell SIB device.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrmn9IjZjUxjbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim6McJQWponA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFGiurvO&amp;md5=c41ca45b344029cc642cb5324c0a3d0c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201602898"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Anode Materials for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">2000927</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.202000927</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Heteroatom Doping: An Effective Way to Boost Sodium Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yu; Chen, Minghua; Liu, Bo; Zhang, Yan; Liang, Xinqi; Xia, Xinhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">27</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000927</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  In response to the change of energy landscape, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are becoming one of the most promising power sources for the post-lithium-ion battery (LIB) era due to the cheap and abundant nature of sodium, and similar electrochem. properties to LIBs.  The electrochem. performance of electrode materials for SIBs is closely bound up with their crystal structures and intrinsic electronic/ionic states.  Apart from nanoscale design and conductive composite strategies, heteroatom doping is another effective way to enhance the intrinsic transfer characteristics of sodium ions and electrons in crystal structures to accelerate reaction kinetics and thereby achieve high performance.  In this review, the recent advancements in heteroatom doping for sodium ion storage of electrode materials are reviewed.  Specifically, different doping strategies including nonmetal element doping (e.g., nitrogen, sulfur, phosphorous, boron, fluorine), metal element doping (magnesium, titanium, iron, aluminum, nickel, copper, etc.), and dual/triple doping (such as N-S, N-P, N-S-P) are reviewed and summarized in detail.  Furthermore, various doping methods are introduced and their advantages and disadvantages are discussed.  The doping effect on crystal structure and intrinsic electronic/ionic state are illustrated and the relationship with capacity and energy/power d. is interrogated.  Finally, future development trends in doping strategies for advanced SIBs electrodes are analyzed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoca4zmQM8fj7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim6McJQWponA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtVyrsb7N&amp;md5=178aa553953d90a51a9b53b47bd04654</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2020.12.010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mao, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">PVP-assisted Synthesis of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>-derived N-doped Graphene with Tunable Interplanar Spacing as High-performance Lithium/sodium Ions Battery Anodes</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>174</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">98</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">109</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon materials have attracted abundant attentions and been utilized widely as anodes in rechargeable Li+/Na+/K+ ions batteries.  Regarding the various radius of alkali-metal-ions, reversible intercalation and deintercalation in interlayers of carbons requires different interplanar spacing.  In this work, N-doped graphene with tunable interplanar spacing is synthesized using graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) as precursor with the assistance of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP).  The obtained tunable interplanar spacing in the range from 0.34-0.45 nm with enlarged values is mainly ascribed to the high nitrogen doping levels (9.98-33.7 at. %), esp. to the pyrrolic nitrogen.  Serving as the anode materials in lithium ions batteries (LIBs) and sodium ions batteries (SIBs), promising specific capacities of 1236 mAh g-1 for LIBs and 300 mAh g-1 for SIBs at c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and excellent cycling stability as well as acceptable rate capability are demonstrated for the resultant N-doped graphene.  The effect of interplanar spacing on the electrochem. performances of LIBs and SIBs are investigated comparatively.  This work outlines a facial route to synthesize N-doped graphene with tunable interplanar spacing, meeting the various requirements unitized as anodes in rechargeable Li+/Na+/K+ ions batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrS4Gw3IeSu67Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim6McJQWponA"> &gt;&gt; To address these issues, herein a biomass-deriving method is reported to achieve the in situ phosphorus doping (P-doping) of carbon dots and their simultaneous decoration onto graphene matrix. Finally, an all-solid-state flexible Al-air battery is designed and fabricated based on our new nanocomposites.  The device exhibits a stable discharge voltage of ≈1.2 V upon different bending states.  This study introduces a unique biomass-derived material system to replace the noble metal catalysts for future portable and wearable electronic devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqWiV_-P6zS_bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim6McJQWponA"> &gt;&gt;             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The main challenge assocd. with sodium-ion battery (SIB) anodes is a search for novel candidate materials with high capacity and excellent rate capability.  The most commonly used and effective route for graphene-based anode design is the introduction of in-plane "hole" defects via nitrogen-doping; this creates a spacious reservoir for storing more energy.  Inspired by mountains in nature, herein, we propose another way - the introduction of blistering in graphene instead of making "holes"; this facilitates adsorbing/inserting more Na+ ions.  In order to properly answer the key question: ""protrusions" or "holes" in graphene, which is better for sodium ion storage", two types of anode materials with a similar doping level were designed: a phosphorus-doped graphene (GP, with protrusions) and a nitrogen-doped graphene (GN, with holes).  As compared with GN, the GP anode perfectly satisfies all the desired criteria: it reveals an ultrahigh capacity (374 mA h g-1 after 120 cycles at 25 mA g-1) comparable to the best graphite anodes in a std. Li-ion battery (∼372 mA h g-1), and exhibits an excellent rate capability (210 mA h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).  In situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) expts. and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. were utilized to uncover the origin of the enhanced electrochem. activity of "protrusions" compared to "holes" in SIBs, down to the at. scale.  The introduction of protrusions through P-doping into graphene is envisaged to be a novel effective way to enhance the capacity and rate performance of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZF4LZN3ZV3LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim6McJQWponA"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Low-cost sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have shown hopeful perspective for large-scale energy storage systems.  However, the development of carbon anode materials with high gravimetric and volumetric capacity is still a challenge.  Herein, we propose a novel dense and N, P co-doped carbon derived from high d. crosslinked bacterial cellulose (BC) with hexachlorocyclotriphosphazene (HCCP), which simultaneously combines high gravimetric and volumetric capacity.  The as-prepd. electrode demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 223 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1 and superior rate performance (145 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1) based on the overall electrode materials.  The full SIBs with this electrode show a high energy d. (156.1 Wh kg-1) as well as a high Coulombic efficiency in the potential range of 0-4.2 V.  D. functional theory (DFT) calcn. and kinetic anal. reveals that the synergistic effect of N, P co-doped configurations contributes to the superior sodium storage performance.  Moreover, this electrode possesses high pressing d. of 1.40 g cm-3, which increases the volumetric capacity greatly (from 97.2 to 312.2 mAh cm-3).  This work proposes a new idea towards the development of low-cost bacterial cellulose that can be used in practical applications for high gravimetric and volumetric performance SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp2aPMIsc74nrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim6McJQWponA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXoslejtr8%253D&amp;md5=b36ca0d77510ec6dac9938b3e4792155</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.jmst.2020.11.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Experimental Design and Theoretical Evaluation of Nitrogen and Phosphorus Dual-doped Hierarchical Porous Carbon for High-performance Sodium-ion Storage</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Starch has a wide range of sources and can be used as a high-quality precursor for sodium-ion battery anode materials.  However, the carbonization yield and specific capacity of carbon materials obtained by directly pyrolyzing starch are low.  Herein, starch is used as the carbon source, and ammonium polyphosphate (APP) is used as the crosslinking agent and dopant to prep. a nitrogen and phosphorus co-doped porous carbon (NPPC).  As the anode for sodium-ion batteries, NPPC-2 exhibits a high reversible capacity of 385.8 mA h g-1 at 50 mA g-1.  Even after 1000 cycles at a large c.d. of 5 A g-1, the reversible capacity can still be maintained at 126.9 mA h g-1.  Based on detailed data and first-principles calcns., the excellent performance of NPPC is due to the effective doping of nitrogen and phosphorus elements, which distorts the graphite sheet, introduces defects, and increases the graphite layer spacing, thereby enhancing the adsorption capacity of the carbon material for sodium ions, reducing the diffusion barrier of sodium ions.  This work provides a new idea for heteroatom doping and carbon material modification.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGplKgqpTESGPrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljZHaiwkEHolQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XhtFehtrfL&amp;md5=2dae9702ee3c3f14be2adaf188438a76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2020.146221"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Chemical Grafting-Derived N, P Co-doped Hollow Microporous Carbon Spheres for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes</span>. <i>Appl. Herein, a hollow porous carbon sphere (NP-HPCS) that exhibits a high N, P co-doping level (6.05 at% for N, 5.19 at% for P) and a large microporous specific area (339 m2 g-1) is reported.  The microporous and N, P co-doping synergistic construction of the NP-HPCS was achieved by a high-efficiency chem. grafting of hexachlorocyclotriphosphazene (HCCP) onto polydopamine (PDA) shell, accompanied by the in situ micellar transformation of pore-forming agent F127 in the grafting process.  The as-prepd. NP-HPCS used as an anode for SIBs delivers a superior rate capacity (∼101 mAh g-1 at 20 A g-1) and an excellent long cycle performance (∼120 mAh g-1 after 5000 cycles at 5 A g-1). The work suggests an effective way to develop high-performance carbon anode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKxeDfGe8jOrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljZHaiwkEHolQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXmtlCjurw%253D&amp;md5=fd58ea208f6b0b0a559eb094b01bb7a6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA04169D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Panja, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bhattacharjya, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nitrogen and Phosphorus Co-doped Cubic Ordered Mesoporous Carbon as a Supercapacitor Electrode Material with Extraordinary Cyclic Stability</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Heteroatoms and porosity both have different, but definite effects on the electrochem. capacitance of C materials.  These effects are studied in detail by using cubic ordered mesoporous carbons (OMCs) co-doped with N and P. 3-Dimensional (3D) mesoporous SiO2, KIT-6, with bicontinuous cubic Ia3d symmetry is used as a hard template to synthesize the cubic OMCs.  Although the porosity parameters e.g. surface area and pore vol. do not change much with N doping, a significant increase of these values is obsd. upon P doping.  Also, the P content does not affect the N doping characteristics on co-doping of both N and P.  When tested as a supercapacitor electrode, the N-OMC, despite its much lower porosity parameters, exhibits a similar specific capacitance to that of the P-OMC.  The high specific capacitance of N-OMC arises mainly from the pseudocapacitive effect of doped N species, whereas high porosity parameters are the main reason for the specific capacitance shown by P-OMC.  The synergistic contribution of both effects enables the NP co-doped OMC to show the highest specific capacitance of 210 F g-1 at 1.0 A g-1.  Also, excellent retention of specific capacitance with &gt;90% of initial capacitance is obsd. for NP-OMC at a high c.d. of 10 A g-1 and also for 3000 charge-discharge cycles.  This is mainly because of high-surface area hierarchical porous structures with uniform and ordered mesopores in the cubic OMC, which facilitate the unrestricted movement of electrolyte ions to access the active surfaces, as well as the excellent synergistic effect of co-doping of N and P.  This is also supported by electrochem. impedance spectroscopic anal., which shows negligible mass transfer resistance and internal cell resistance.  Overall, the synthesized cubic OMC materials are highly promising as electrodes for supercapacitors and other energy-related applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmFegT-urn7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljZHaiwkEHolQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1ajurvJ&amp;md5=c54602ceee540c571b37302a19103687</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1039/C9TA08151H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yun, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Three-dimensional Nitrogen and Phosphorus Co-doped carbon Quantum Dots/reduced Graphene Oxide Composite Aerogels with a Hierarchical Porous Structure as Superior Electrode Materials for Supercapacitors</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">C quantum dot (CQD)-based electrode materials as a novel kind of emerging materials have recently received increasing interest owing to their many unique properties.  Here, graphitic CQDs are synthesized by a modified chem. oxidn. approach, and further utilized as the intercalator to prep. novel 3-dimensional (3D) N, P co-doped CQDs/reduced graphene oxide (rGO) composite aerogels (N, P-CQDs/rGO) by a 1-step hydrothermal approach.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Heteroatom-doped porous carbon materials (HPCMs) have found extensive applications in adsorption/sepn., org. catalysis, sensing, and energy conversion/storage.  The judicious choice of carbon precursors is crucial for the manuf. of HPCMs with specific usages and maximization of their functions.  In this regard, polymers as precursors have demonstrated great promise because of their versatile mol. and nanoscale structures, modulatable chem. compn., and rich processing techniques to generate textures that, in combination with proper solid-state chem., can be maintained throughout carbonization.  This Review comprehensively surveys the progress in polymer-derived functional HPCMs in terms of how to produce and control their porosities, heteroatom doping effects, and morphologies and their related use.  First, we summarize and discuss synthetic approaches, including hard and soft templating methods as well as direct synthesis strategies employing polymers to control the pores and/or heteroatoms in HPCMs.  Second, we summarize the heteroatom doping effects on the thermal stability, electronic and optical properties, and surface chem. of HPCMs.  Specifically, the heteroatom doping effect, which involves both single-type heteroatom doping and codoping of two or more types of heteroatoms into the carbon network, is discussed.  Considering the significance of the morphologies of HPCMs in their application spectrum, potential choices of suitable polymeric precursors and strategies to precisely regulate the morphologies of HPCMs are presented.  Finally, we provide our perspective on how to predefine the structures of HPCMs by using polymers to realize their potential applications in the current fields of energy generation/conversion and environmental remediation.  We believe that these analyses and deductions are valuable for a systematic understanding of polymer-derived carbon materials and will serve as a source of inspiration for the design of future HPCMs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoPdxYEZ2ZYL7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljZHaiwkEHolQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFahu7bF&amp;md5=3369bd71795e4c4687da52bf9492ea92</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2020.07.021"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, D.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, O.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbonaceous materials are the most promising anode materials in electrochem. energy storage systems. By controlling the P configuration, the P-HCNs03 exhibits reversible capacity as high as 260 mA h g-1 at the c.d. of 1.0 A g-1 after 500 cycles with an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 73%.  When the amt. of phosphorus in the as-prepd. materials is changed, the different structures of the P-doped carbon lattices are analyzed by X-ray diffraction, Raman spectroscopy, and XPS.  Based on the first-principles calcn., although the P-O bond has the most configurations, the excellent reversible capacity of the electrode is attributed to the strong Na-absorption ability of P=O and P-C bonds.  The sodium-based dual-ion batteries (NDIBs) assembled with P-HCNs03 as an anode and expanded graphite as a cathode (P-HCNs03//EG) exhibited a high energy d. of 138 W h kg-1 at a power d. of 159 W kg-1.  The results provide an important angle to optimize the performance of hard carbons with other functionalized heteroatoms.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoLzU_tAWoP3bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKhvbbAsN7eg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXlsVOmu7g%253D&amp;md5=b1b27ec5fdaa7dc30a023c47d2377669</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b05618"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Phosphorus-doped Hard Carbon Nanofibers Prepared by Electrospinning as an Anode in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus-doped hard carbon nanofibers with macroporous structure were successfully synthesized by electrospinning followed by a thermal treatment process using polyacrylonitrile and H3PO4 as carbon and phosphorus precursors, resp.  XPS anal. reveals that the doped phosphorus atoms can incorporate into the carbon framework and most of them are connecting with carbon atoms to form P-C bond.  The (002) plane interlayer spacing was taken from the X-ray diffraction pattern, which shows a large spacing of 3.83 Å for the obtained P-doped hard carbon nanofibers.  When used as an anode in sodium-ion batteries, the as-prepd. P-doped hard carbon nanofibers can deliver a reversible capacity of 288 and 103 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 and 2 A g-1, resp.  After 200 cycles at 50 mA g-1, the capacity retention of P-doped hard carbon nanofibers still reaches 87.8%, demonstrating good cycling durability.  These excellent electrochem. performances of P-doped hard carbon nanofibers can be attributed to the macroporous structure, large interlayer spacing, and the formation of P-C bond.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQsDJ4Qu6jkrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKhvbbAsN7eg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtVKgtLjN&amp;md5=f7fe4c603207e1d5f8ab260923b0fbfa</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA06113J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nie, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Porous Nitrogen and Phosphorus Co-Doped Carbon Nanofiber Networks for High Performance Electrical Double Layer Capacitors</span>. <i>J.
